<p><b>Cairo</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">KY</span>-roh</i></a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">القاهرة</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Arabic" title="Romanization of Arabic">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">al-Qāhirah</i></span>, <small>pronounced </small><span class="IPA" lang="arz-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Egyptian Arabic">[ælˈqɑ(ː)heɾɑ]</a></span>) is the capital of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_cities_in_the_Arab_world" title="List of largest cities in the Arab world">largest city in the Arab world</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Greater_Cairo" title="Greater Cairo">Greater Cairo</a> metropolitan area, with a population of 21.3 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_urban_agglomerations_in_Africa" title="List of urban agglomerations in Africa">2nd largest urban agglomeration in Africa</a>, the largest in the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_metropolitan_areas_of_the_Middle_East" title="List of largest metropolitan areas of the Middle East">the Middle East</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_cities#List" title="List of largest cities">sixth-largest in the world by population</a>. Cairo is associated with <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a>, as the <a href="/wiki/Giza_pyramid_complex" title="Giza pyramid complex">Giza pyramid complex</a> and the ancient cities of <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">Memphis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heliopolis_(ancient_Egypt)" title="Heliopolis (ancient Egypt)">Heliopolis</a> are located in its geographical area. Located near the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forbes12_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-forbes12-12">[11]</a></sup> the city first developed as <a href="/wiki/Fustat" title="Fustat">Fustat</a>, a settlement founded after the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Muslim conquest of Egypt">Muslim conquest of Egypt</a> in 640 next to an existing ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_empire" title="Roman empire">Roman</a> fortress, <a href="/wiki/Babylon_Fortress" title="Babylon Fortress">Babylon</a>. Under the <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid dynasty</a> a new city, <i>al-Qāhirah</i>, was founded nearby in 969. It later superseded Fustat as the main urban centre during the <a href="/wiki/Ayyubid_dynasty" title="Ayyubid dynasty">Ayyubid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate" title="Mamluk Sultanate">Mamluk</a> periods (12th–16th centuries).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond199383-85_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond199383-85-13">[12]</a></sup> Cairo has long been a centre of the region's political and cultural life, and is titled "the city of a thousand <a href="/wiki/Minaret" title="Minaret">minarets</a>" for its preponderance of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic architecture</a>. Cairo's <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Cairo" title="Islamic Cairo">historic center</a> was awarded <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>-status in 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-14">[13]</a></sup> Cairo is considered a <a href="/wiki/Global_city" title="Global city">World City</a> with a "Beta +" classification according to <a href="/wiki/Globalization_and_World_Cities_Research_Network" title="Globalization and World Cities Research Network">GaWC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, Cairo has the oldest and largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_cinema" title="Egyptian cinema">cinema</a> and <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Egypt" title="Music of Egypt">music industry</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_World" title="Arab World">Arab World</a>, as well as the world's second-oldest institution of higher learning, <a href="/wiki/Al-Azhar_University" title="Al-Azhar University">Al-Azhar University</a>. Many international media, businesses, and organizations have regional headquarters in the city; the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> has had its headquarters in Cairo for most of its existence.
</p><p>With a population of over 10 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAPMAS_-_Egypt_in_Figures_2017_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-CAPMAS_-_Egypt_in_Figures_2017-16">[15]</a></sup> spread over 453 km<sup>2</sup> (175 sq mi), Cairo is by far the largest city in Egypt. An additional 9.5 million inhabitants live in close proximity to the city. Cairo, like many other <a href="/wiki/Megacity" title="Megacity">megacities</a>, suffers from high levels of pollution and traffic. The <a href="/wiki/Cairo_Metro" title="Cairo Metro">Cairo Metro</a>, opened in 1987, is the oldest <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">metro system</a> in Africa,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> and ranks amongst the fifteen busiest in the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> with over 1 billion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> annual passenger rides. The economy of Cairo was ranked first in the Middle East in 2005,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> and 43rd globally on <i><a href="/wiki/Foreign_Policy" title="Foreign Policy">Foreign Policy</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> 2010 <a href="/wiki/Global_city" title="Global city">Global Cities Index</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cairo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Egyptians often refer to Cairo as <i><span title="Egyptian Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="arz-Latn">Maṣr</i></span></i> (<small>IPA: </small><span class="IPA" lang="arz-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Egyptian Arabic">[mɑsˤɾ]</a></span>; <span title="Egyptian Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="arz">مَصر</span></span>), the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Egyptian Arabic">Egyptian Arabic</a> name for Egypt itself, emphasizing the city's importance for the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBehrens-Abouseif19928_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBehrens-Abouseif19928-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGolia2004152_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGolia2004152-23">[22]</a></sup> Its official name <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">al-Qāhirah </i></span></i> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">القاهرة</span></span>) means 'the Vanquisher' or 'the Conqueror, supposedly due to the fact that the planet <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, <span title="Arabic-language text"><i lang="ar-Latn">an-Najm al-Qāhir</i></span> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">النجم القاهر</span></span>, 'the Conquering Star'), was rising at the time when the city was founded,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> possibly also in reference to the much awaited arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid</a> Caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Mu%27izz_li-Din_Allah" title="Al-Mu'izz li-Din Allah">Al-Mu'izz</a> who reached Cairo in 973 from <a href="/wiki/Mahdia" title="Mahdia">Mahdia</a>, the old Fatimid capital. The location of the ancient city of <a href="/wiki/Heliopolis_(ancient_Egypt)" title="Heliopolis (ancient Egypt)">Heliopolis</a> is the suburb of <a href="/wiki/Ain_Shams" title="Ain Shams">Ain Shams</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">عين شمس</span>, 'Eye of the Sun').
</p><p>There are a few <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a> names of the city. <i>Ti•kash•roomi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>: <span lang="cop">Ϯⲕⲉϣⲣⲱⲙⲓ</span> <small>Late Coptic:</small> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[di.kɑʃˈɾoːmi]</a></span>) is attested in the 1211 text <i>The Martyrdom of John of Phanijoit</i> and is either a calque meaning 'man breaker' (<span title="Coptic-language text"><span lang="cop">Ϯ-</span></span>, 'the', <span title="Coptic-language text"><span lang="cop">ⲕⲁϣ-</span></span>, 'to break', and <span title="Coptic-language text"><span lang="cop">ⲣⲱⲙⲓ</span></span>, 'man'), akin to Arabic <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">al-Qāhirah</i></span></i>, or a derivation from <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">قَصْر الرُوم</span></span> (<i>qaṣr ar-rūm</i>, “the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> castle”), another name of <a href="/wiki/Babylon_Fortress" title="Babylon Fortress">Babylon Fortress</a> in <a href="/wiki/Old_Cairo" title="Old Cairo">Old Cairo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-25">[24]</a></sup> The form Khairon (<a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>: <span lang="cop">ⲭⲁⲓⲣⲟⲛ</span>) is attested in the modern Coptic text Ⲡⲓⲫⲓⲣⲓ ⲛ̀ⲧⲉ ϯⲁⲅⲓⲁ ⲙ̀ⲙⲏⲓ Ⲃⲉⲣⲏⲛⲁ (The tale of <a href="/wiki/Verena_of_Zurzach" title="Verena of Zurzach">Saint Verina</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="The current source is insufficiently reliable (WP:NOTRS). More clarity is also needed on the origin and relevance of the &quot;modern Coptic text&quot; noted here; if the text in question is merely this anonymous webpage, then it's not noteworthy here. (June 2022)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <span title="Coptic-language romanization"><i lang="cop-Latn">Lioui</i></span> (<span title="Coptic-language text"><span lang="cop">Ⲗⲓⲟⲩⲓ</span></span> <small>Late Coptic:</small> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[lɪˈjuːj]</a></span>) or <span title="Coptic-language romanization"><i lang="cop-Latn">Elioui</i></span> (<span title="Coptic-language text"><span lang="cop">Ⲉⲗⲓⲟⲩⲓ</span></span> <small>Late Coptic:</small> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[ælˈjuːj]</a></span>) is another name which is descended from the Greek name of Heliopolis (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Ήλιούπολις</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-25">[24]</a></sup> Some argue that <span title="Coptic-language romanization"><i lang="cop-Latn">Mistram</i></span> (<span title="Coptic-language text"><span lang="cop">Ⲙⲓⲥⲧⲣⲁⲙ</span></span> <small>Late Coptic:</small> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[ˈmɪs.təɾɑm]</a></span>) or <span title="Coptic-language romanization"><i lang="cop-Latn">Nistram</i></span> (<span title="Coptic-language text"><span lang="cop">Ⲛⲓⲥⲧⲣⲁⲙ</span></span> <small>Late Coptic:</small> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[ˈnɪs.təɾɑm]</a></span>) is another Coptic name for Cairo, although others think that it's rather a name of an <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid</a> capital <a href="/wiki/Al-Askar" title="Al-Askar">Al-Askar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-27">[26]</a></sup> <span title="Coptic-language text"><span lang="cop">Ⲕⲁϩⲓⲣⲏ</span></span> (<span title="Coptic-language text"><i lang="cop-Latn">Kahi•ree</i></span>) is a popular modern rendering of an Arabic name (others being <span title="Coptic-language text"><span lang="cop">Ⲕⲁⲓⲣⲟⲛ</span></span> [Kairon] and <span title="Coptic-language text"><span lang="cop">Ⲕⲁϩⲓⲣⲁ</span></span> [Kahira]) which is modern folk etymology meaning 'land of sun'. Some argue that it was a name of an Egyptian settlement upon which Cairo was built, but it's rather doubtful as this name is not attested in any <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_hieroglyphs" title="Egyptian hieroglyphs">Hieroglyphic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Demotic_(Egyptian)" title="Demotic (Egyptian)">Demotic</a> source, although some researchers, like Paul Casanova, view it as a legitimate theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-25">[24]</a></sup> Cairo is also referred to as <span title="Coptic-language text"><span lang="cop">Ⲭⲏⲙⲓ</span></span> (<small>Late Coptic:</small> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[ˈkɪ.mi]</a></span>) or <span title="Coptic-language text"><span lang="cop">Ⲅⲩⲡⲧⲟⲥ</span></span> (<small>Late Coptic:</small> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[ˈɡɪp.dos]</a></span>), which means Egypt in Coptic, the same way it's referred to in Egyptian Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sometimes the city is informally referred to as <i><span title="Egyptian Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="arz-Latn">Cairo</i></span></i> by people from Alexandria (<small>IPA: </small><span class="IPA" lang="arz-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Egyptian Arabic">[ˈkæjɾo]</a></span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic_language" title="Egyptian Arabic language">Egyptian Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="arz">كايرو</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cairo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Egypt" title="History of Egypt">History of Egypt</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a chronological guide, see <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Cairo" title="Timeline of Cairo">Timeline of Cairo</a>.</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_settlements">Ancient settlements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cairo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ancient settlements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cairo_-_Coptic_area_-_Roman_Tower.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2246" data-file-width="2328" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Cairo_-_Coptic_area_-_Roman_Tower.JPG/220px-Cairo_-_Coptic_area_-_Roman_Tower.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Cairo_-_Coptic_area_-_Roman_Tower.JPG/330px-Cairo_-_Coptic_area_-_Roman_Tower.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Cairo_-_Coptic_area_-_Roman_Tower.JPG/440px-Cairo_-_Coptic_area_-_Roman_Tower.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cairo_-_Coptic_area_-_Roman_Tower.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Remains of a circular Roman tower at the <a href="/wiki/Babylon_Fortress" title="Babylon Fortress">Babylon Fortress</a> (late 3rd century) in <a href="/wiki/Old_Cairo" title="Old Cairo">Old Cairo</a></div></div></div>
<p>The area around present-day Cairo had long been a focal point of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> due to its strategic location at the junction of the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile Valley</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a> regions (roughly <a href="/wiki/Upper_Egypt" title="Upper Egypt">Upper Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lower_Egypt" title="Lower Egypt">Lower Egypt</a>), which also placed it at the crossing of major routes between <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabravan_LoonReifSwelim201318_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabravan_LoonReifSwelim201318-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-:24-30">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">Memphis</a>, the capital of Egypt during the <a href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Old Kingdom of Egypt">Old Kingdom</a> and a major city up until the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic period</a>, was located a short distance south of present-day Cairo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-31">[30]</a></sup> Heliopolis, another important city and major religious center, was located in what are now the northeastern suburbs of Cairo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-31">[30]</a></sup> It was largely destroyed by the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Achaemenid conquest of Egypt">Persian invasions</a> in 525 BC and 343 BC and partly abandoned by the late first century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabravan_LoonReifSwelim201318_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabravan_LoonReifSwelim201318-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the origins of modern Cairo are generally traced back to a series of settlements in the first millennium AD. Around the turn of the fourth century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHawassBrock2003456_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHawassBrock2003456-32">[31]</a></sup> as Memphis was continuing to decline in importance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Romans</a> established a large fortress along the east bank of the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>. The fortress, called <a href="/wiki/Babylon_Fortress" title="Babylon Fortress">Babylon</a>, was built by the Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a> (r. 285–305) at the entrance of a canal connecting the Nile to the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> that was created earlier by emperor <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a> (r. 98–115).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[b]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabravan_LoonReifSwelim201320–22_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabravan_LoonReifSwelim201320–22-35">[33]</a></sup> Further north of the fortress, near the present-day district of <a href="/wiki/Azbakeya" title="Azbakeya">al-Azbakiya</a>, was a <a href="/wiki/Port" title="Port">port</a> and fortified outpost known as Tendunyas (<a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>: <span lang="cop">ϯⲁⲛⲧⲱⲛⲓⲁⲥ</span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup> or Umm Dunayn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbu-Lughod19716_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbu-Lughod19716-38">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> While no structures older than the 7th century have been preserved in the area aside from the Roman fortifications, historical evidence suggests that a sizeable city existed. The city was important enough that its <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishop</a>, Cyrus, participated in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Ephesus" title="Second Council of Ephesus">Second Council of Ephesus</a> in 449.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabravan_LoonReifSwelim201333_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabravan_LoonReifSwelim201333-40">[38]</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Sasanian_War_of_602%E2%80%93628" title="Byzantine–Sasanian War of 602–628">Byzantine-Sassanian War</a> between 602 and 628 caused great hardship and likely caused much of the urban population to leave for the countryside, leaving the settlement partly deserted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbu-Lughod19716_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbu-Lughod19716-38">[36]</a></sup> The site today remains at the nucleus of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox" title="Coptic Orthodox">Coptic Orthodox</a> community, which separated from the Roman and Byzantine churches in the late 4th century. Cairo's oldest extant churches, such as the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Barbara_Church_in_Coptic_Cairo" title="Saint Barbara Church in Coptic Cairo">Church of Saint Barbara</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saints_Sergius_and_Bacchus_Church_(Abu_Serga)" title="Saints Sergius and Bacchus Church (Abu Serga)">Church of Saints Sergius and Bacchus</a> (from the late 7th or early 8th century), are located inside the fortress walls in what is now known as <a href="/wiki/Old_Cairo" title="Old Cairo">Old Cairo</a> or <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Cairo" title="Coptic Cairo">Coptic Cairo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabravan_LoonReifSwelim201375_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabravan_LoonReifSwelim201375-41">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fustat_and_other_early_Islamic_settlements">Fustat and other early Islamic settlements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cairo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Fustat and other early Islamic settlements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CairoFustatHouses.jpg"><img alt="A man on a donkey walks past a palm tree, with a mosque and market behind Mohamed kamal" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/CairoFustatHouses.jpg/220px-CairoFustatHouses.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/CairoFustatHouses.jpg/330px-CairoFustatHouses.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/CairoFustatHouses.jpg/440px-CairoFustatHouses.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CairoFustatHouses.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Excavated ruins of <a href="/wiki/Fustat" title="Fustat">Fustat</a> (2004 photo)</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Muslim conquest of Egypt">Muslim conquest of Byzantine Egypt</a> was led by <a href="/wiki/Amr_ibn_al-As" title="Amr ibn al-As">Amr ibn al-As</a> from 639 to 642. Babylon Fortress was besieged in September 640 and fell in April 641. In 641 or early 642, after the surrender of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> (the Egyptian capital at the time), he founded a new settlement next to the Babylon Fortress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond199316-18_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond199316-18-42">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKennedy2007161_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedy2007161-43">[41]</a></sup> The city, known as <a href="/wiki/Fustat" title="Fustat">Fustat</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">الفسطاط</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Arabic" title="Romanization of Arabic">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">al-Fusṭāṭ</i></span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'the tent'), served as a garrison town and as the new administrative capital of Egypt. Historians such as <a href="/wiki/Janet_Abu-Lughod" title="Janet Abu-Lughod">Janet Abu-Lughod</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Raymond" title="André Raymond">André Raymond</a> trace the genesis of present-day Cairo to the foundation of Fustat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond199313_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond199313-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbu-Lughod19717_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbu-Lughod19717-45">[43]</a></sup> The choice of founding a new settlement at this inland location, instead of using the existing capital of Alexandria on the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> coast, may have been due to the new conquerors' strategic priorities. One of the first projects of the new Muslim administration was to clear and re-open Trajan's ancient canal in order to ship grain more directly from Egypt to <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">caliphate</a> in <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond199319_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond199319-46">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKennedy2007160_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedy2007160-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlSayyad201142_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlSayyad201142-48">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabravan_LoonReifSwelim201321_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabravan_LoonReifSwelim201321-49">[47]</a></sup> Ibn al-As also founded a mosque for the city at the same time, now known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosque_of_Amr_ibn_al-As" title="Mosque of Amr ibn al-As">Mosque of Amr Ibn al-As</a>, the oldest mosque in Egypt and Africa (although the current structure dates from later expansions).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-:24-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabravan_LoonReifSwelim2013280_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabravan_LoonReifSwelim2013280-50">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 750, following the overthrow of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umayyad_caliphate" title="Umayyad caliphate">Umayyad caliphate</a> by the Abbasids, the new rulers created their own settlement to the northeast of Fustat which became the new provincial capital. This was known as <a href="/wiki/Al-Askar" title="Al-Askar">al-Askar</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">العسكر</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'the camp') as it was laid out like a military camp. A governor's residence and a new mosque were also added, with the latter completed in 786.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond199330-31_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond199330-31-53">[51]</a></sup> In 861, on the orders of the Abbasid caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Mutawakkil" title="Al-Mutawakkil">al-Mutawakkil</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Nilometer" title="Nilometer">Nilometer</a> was built on <a href="/wiki/Roda_Island" title="Roda Island">Roda Island</a> near Fustat. Although it was repaired and given a new roof in later centuries, its basic structure is still preserved today, making it the oldest preserved Islamic-era structure in Cairo today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams201842_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams201842-54">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBehrens-Abouseif199250_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBehrens-Abouseif199250-55">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kairo_Ibn_Tulun_Moschee_BW_5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2378" data-file-width="3641" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Kairo_Ibn_Tulun_Moschee_BW_5.jpg/220px-Kairo_Ibn_Tulun_Moschee_BW_5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Kairo_Ibn_Tulun_Moschee_BW_5.jpg/330px-Kairo_Ibn_Tulun_Moschee_BW_5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Kairo_Ibn_Tulun_Moschee_BW_5.jpg/440px-Kairo_Ibn_Tulun_Moschee_BW_5.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kairo_Ibn_Tulun_Moschee_BW_5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Mosque_of_Ibn_Tulun" title="Mosque of Ibn Tulun">Mosque of Ibn Tulun</a>, built by <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Tulun" title="Ahmad ibn Tulun">Ahmad Ibn Tulun</a> in 876–879 AD</div></div></div>
<p>In 868 a commander of <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> origin named Bakbak was sent to Egypt by the Abbasid caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Mu%27tazz" title="Al-Mu'tazz">al-Mu'taz</a> to restore order after a rebellion in the country. He was accompanied by his stepson, <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Tulun" title="Ahmad ibn Tulun">Ahmad ibn Tulun</a>, who became effective governor of Egypt. Over time, Ibn Tulun gained an army and accumulated influence and wealth, allowing him to become the <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> independent ruler of both Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Syria_(region)" title="Syria (region)">Syria</a> by 878.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwelim201528–32_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwelim201528–32-56">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond199333_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond199333-57">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:242_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-:242-58">[56]</a></sup> In 870, he used his growing wealth to found a new administrative capital, <a href="/wiki/Al-Qata%27i" title="Al-Qata'i">al-Qata'i</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">القطائـع</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'the allotments'), to the northeast of Fustat and of al-Askar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:242_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-:242-58">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwelim201537_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwelim201537-59">[57]</a></sup> The new city included a palace known as the <i>Dar al-Imara</i>, a parade ground known as <i>al-Maydan</i>, a <a href="/wiki/Bimaristan" title="Bimaristan">bimaristan</a> (hospital), and an <a href="/wiki/Aqueduct_(water_supply)" title="Aqueduct (water supply)">aqueduct</a> to supply water. Between 876 and 879 Ibn Tulun built a great mosque, now known as the <a href="/wiki/Mosque_of_Ibn_Tulun" title="Mosque of Ibn Tulun">Mosque of Ibn Tulun</a>, at the center of the city, next to the palace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond199333_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond199333-57">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwelim201537_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwelim201537-59">[57]</a></sup> After his death in 884, Ibn Tulun was succeeded by his son and his descendants who continued a short-lived dynasty, the <a href="/wiki/Tulunids" title="Tulunids">Tulunids</a>. In 905, the Abbasids sent general Muhammad Sulayman al-Katib to re-assert direct control over the country. Tulunid rule was ended and al-Qatta'i was razed to the ground, except for the mosque which remains standing today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwelim201532–37_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwelim201532–37-60">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond199334_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond199334-61">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundation_and_expansion_of_Cairo">Foundation and expansion of Cairo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cairo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Foundation and expansion of Cairo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Egypt_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Egypt in the Middle Ages">Egypt in the Middle Ages</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_story_of_Cairo_(1906)_(14782234955).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2200" data-file-width="1676" decoding="async" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/The_story_of_Cairo_%281906%29_%2814782234955%29.jpg/220px-The_story_of_Cairo_%281906%29_%2814782234955%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/The_story_of_Cairo_%281906%29_%2814782234955%29.jpg/330px-The_story_of_Cairo_%281906%29_%2814782234955%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/The_story_of_Cairo_%281906%29_%2814782234955%29.jpg/440px-The_story_of_Cairo_%281906%29_%2814782234955%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_story_of_Cairo_(1906)_(14782234955).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A plan of Cairo before 1200 AD, as reconstructed by <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Lane-Poole" title="Stanley Lane-Poole">Stanley Lane-Poole</a> (1906), showing the location of Fatimid structures, Saladin's <a href="/wiki/Cairo_Citadel" title="Cairo Citadel">Citadel</a>, and earlier sites (Fustat not shown)</div></div></div>
<p>In 969, the <a href="/wiki/Isma%27ilism" title="Isma'ilism">Shi'a Isma'ili</a> Fatimid empire <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Fatimid conquest of Egypt">conquered Egypt</a> after ruling from Ifriqiya. The Fatimid general <a href="/wiki/Jawhar_(general)" title="Jawhar (general)">Jawhar Al Saqili</a> founded a new fortified city northeast of <a href="/wiki/Fustat" title="Fustat">Fustat</a> and of former al-Qata'i. It took four years to build the city, initially known as al-Manṣūriyyah,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlasséSmith200396_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlasséSmith200396-62">[60]</a></sup> which was to serve as the new capital of the caliphate. During that time, the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Al-Azhar_Mosque" title="Al-Azhar Mosque">al-Azhar Mosque</a> was commissioned by order of the caliph, which developed into the third-oldest university in the world. Cairo would eventually become a centre of learning, with the library of Cairo containing hundreds of thousands of books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeriBacharach2006451_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeriBacharach2006451-63">[61]</a></sup> When Caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Mu%27izz_li-Din_Allah" title="Al-Mu'izz li-Din Allah">al-Mu'izz li Din Allah</a> arrived from the old Fatimid capital of <a href="/wiki/Mahdia" title="Mahdia">Mahdia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> in 973, he gave the city its present name, <i>Qāhirat al-Mu'izz</i> ("The Vanquisher of al-Mu'izz"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlasséSmith200396_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlasséSmith200396-62">[60]</a></sup> from which the name "Cairo" (<i>al-Qāhira</i>) originates. The caliphs lived in a vast and lavish <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Great_Palaces" title="Fatimid Great Palaces">palace complex</a> that occupied the heart of the city. Cairo remained a relatively exclusive royal city for most of this era, but during the tenure of <a href="/wiki/Badr_al-Jamali" title="Badr al-Jamali">Badr al-Gamali</a> as <a href="/wiki/Vizier" title="Vizier">vizier</a> (1073–1094) the restrictions were loosened for the first time and richer families from Fustat were allowed to move into the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond199378_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond199378-64">[62]</a></sup> Between 1087 and 1092 Badr al-Gamali also rebuilt the city walls in stone and constructed the city gates of <a href="/wiki/Bab_al-Futuh" title="Bab al-Futuh">Bab al-Futuh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bab_al-Nasr_(Cairo)" title="Bab al-Nasr (Cairo)">Bab al-Nasr</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bab_Zuweila" title="Bab Zuweila">Bab Zuweila</a> that still stand today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond199362-63_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond199362-63-65">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Fatimid period Fustat reached its apogee in size and prosperity, acting as a center of craftsmanship and international trade and as the area's main port on the Nile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond199366-69_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond199366-69-66">[64]</a></sup> Historical sources report that multi-story communal residences existed in the city, particularly in its center, which were typically inhabited by middle and lower-class residents. Some of these were as high as seven stories and could house some 200 to 350 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond200065_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond200065-67">[65]</a></sup> They may have been similar to Roman <i><a href="/wiki/Insula_(Roman_city)" title="Insula (Roman city)">insulae</a></i> and may have been the prototypes for the rental apartment complexes which became common in the later Mamluk and <a href="/wiki/Egypt_Eyalet" title="Egypt Eyalet">Ottoman</a> periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond200065_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond200065-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, in 1168 the Fatimid vizier <a href="/wiki/Shawar" title="Shawar">Shawar</a> set fire to unfortified Fustat to prevent its potential capture by <a href="/wiki/Amalric_of_Jerusalem" title="Amalric of Jerusalem">Amalric</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusader</a> king of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Kingdom of Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>. While the fire did not destroy the city and it continued to exist afterward, it did mark the beginning of its decline. Over the following centuries it was Cairo, the former palace-city, that became the new economic center and attracted migration from Fustat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalyPetry1998213_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalyPetry1998213-68">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond199383–85,_103_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond199383–85,_103-69">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cairo-citadel-1800s.jpg"><img alt="A multi-domed mosque dominates the walled Citadel, with ruined tombs and a lone minaret in front." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2905" data-file-width="3712" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Cairo-citadel-1800s.jpg/220px-Cairo-citadel-1800s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Cairo-citadel-1800s.jpg/330px-Cairo-citadel-1800s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Cairo-citadel-1800s.jpg/440px-Cairo-citadel-1800s.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cairo-citadel-1800s.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Cairo_Citadel" title="Cairo Citadel">Cairo Citadel</a>, seen above in the late 19th century, was begun by <a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">Saladin</a> in 1176</div></div></div>
<p>While the Crusaders did not capture the city in 1168, a continuing power struggle between Shawar, King Amalric, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zengid" title="Zengid">Zengid</a> general <a href="/wiki/Shirkuh" title="Shirkuh">Shirkuh</a> led to the downfall of the Fatimid establishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalyPetry1998213–215_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalyPetry1998213–215-70">[68]</a></sup> In 1169, Shirkuh's nephew <a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">Saladin</a> was appointed as the new vizier of Egypt by the Fatimids and two years later he seized power from the family of the last Fatimid caliph, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-%27%C4%80%E1%B8%8Did" title="Al-'Āḍid">al-'Āḍid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalyPetry1998215_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalyPetry1998215-71">[69]</a></sup> As the first <a href="/wiki/Sultan_of_Egypt" title="Sultan of Egypt">Sultan of Egypt</a>, Saladin established the <a href="/wiki/Ayyubid_dynasty" title="Ayyubid dynasty">Ayyubid dynasty</a>, based in Cairo, and aligned Egypt with the <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a> Abbasids, who were based in <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShillington2005438_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShillington2005438-72">[70]</a></sup> In 1176, Saladin began construction on the <a href="/wiki/Cairo_Citadel" title="Cairo Citadel">Cairo Citadel</a>, which was to serve as the seat of the Egyptian government until the mid-19th century. The construction of the Citadel definitively ended Fatimid-built Cairo's status as an exclusive palace-city and opened it up to common Egyptians and to foreign merchants, spurring its commercial development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-:12-73">[71]</a></sup> Along with the Citadel, Saladin also began the construction of a new 20-kilometre-long wall that would protect both Cairo and Fustat on their eastern side and connect them with the new Citadel. These construction projects continued beyond Saladin's lifetime and were completed under his Ayyubid successors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond199389-97_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond199389-97-74">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Apogee_and_decline_under_the_Mamluks">Apogee and decline under the Mamluks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cairo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Apogee and decline under the Mamluks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cairo,_madrasa_del_sultano_qalaun,_04.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3439" data-file-width="2028" decoding="async" height="373" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Cairo%2C_madrasa_del_sultano_qalaun%2C_04.JPG/220px-Cairo%2C_madrasa_del_sultano_qalaun%2C_04.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Cairo%2C_madrasa_del_sultano_qalaun%2C_04.JPG/330px-Cairo%2C_madrasa_del_sultano_qalaun%2C_04.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Cairo%2C_madrasa_del_sultano_qalaun%2C_04.JPG/440px-Cairo%2C_madrasa_del_sultano_qalaun%2C_04.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cairo,_madrasa_del_sultano_qalaun,_04.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Qalawun_complex" title="Qalawun complex">Mausoleum-Madrasa-Hospital complex</a> of <a href="/wiki/Qalawun" title="Qalawun">Sultan Qalawun</a>, built in 1284–1285 in the center of Cairo, over the remains of a Fatimid palace</div></div></div>
<p>In 1250, during the <a href="/wiki/Seventh_Crusade" title="Seventh Crusade">Seventh Crusade</a>, the Ayyubid dynasty had a crisis with the death of <a href="/wiki/As-Salih_Ayyub" title="As-Salih Ayyub">al-Salih</a> and power transitioned instead to the <a href="/wiki/Mamluk" title="Mamluk">Mamluks</a>, partly with the help of al-Salih's wife, <a href="/wiki/Shajar_al-Durr" title="Shajar al-Durr">Shajar ad-Durr</a>, who ruled for a brief period around this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond1993110_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond1993110-75">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> Mamluks were soldiers who were purchased as young slaves and raised to serve in the sultan's army. Between 1250 and 1517 the throne of the <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate" title="Mamluk Sultanate">Mamluk Sultanate</a> passed from one mamluk to another in a system of succession that was generally non-hereditary, but also frequently violent and chaotic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond1993115–117_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond1993115–117-77">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClot199647-48_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClot199647-48-78">[76]</a></sup> The Mamluk Empire nonetheless became a major power in the region and was responsible for repelling the advance of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongols</a> (most famously at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ain_Jalut" title="Battle of Ain Jalut">Battle of Ain Jalut</a> in 1260) and for eliminating the last <a href="/wiki/Crusader_states" title="Crusader states">Crusader states</a> in the Levant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClot199634-37,_83-107,_112-118_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClot199634-37,_83-107,_112-118-79">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite their military character, the Mamluks were also prolific builders and left a rich <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_architecture" title="Mamluk architecture">architectural legacy</a> throughout Cairo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-80">[78]</a></sup> Continuing a practice started by the Ayyubids, much of the land occupied by former Fatimid palaces was sold and replaced by newer buildings, becoming a prestigious site for the construction of Mamluk religious and funerary complexes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond2000122_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond2000122-81">[79]</a></sup> Construction projects initiated by the Mamluks pushed the city outward while also bringing new infrastructure to the centre of the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond2000120–128_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond2000120–128-82">[80]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Cairo flourished as a centre of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_studies" title="Islamic studies">Islamic scholarship</a> and a crossroads on the <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice trade</a> route among the civilisations in <a href="/wiki/Afro-Eurasia" title="Afro-Eurasia">Afro-Eurasia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShillington2005199_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShillington2005199-83">[81]</a></sup> Under the reign of the Mamluk sultan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/An-Nasir_Muhammad" title="An-Nasir Muhammad">al-Nasir Muhammad</a> (1293–1341, with <a href="/wiki/Interregnum" title="Interregnum">interregnums</a>), Cairo reached its apogee in terms of population and wealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond1993122-124,_140-142_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond1993122-124,_140-142-84">[82]</a></sup> By 1340, Cairo had a population of close to half a million, making it the largest city west of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuan_Dynasty" title="Yuan Dynasty">China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShillington2005199_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShillington2005199-83">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Multi-story buildings occupied by <a href="/wiki/Renting" title="Renting">rental</a> apartments, known as a <i>rab'</i> (plural <i>ribā'</i> or <i>urbu</i>), became common in the Mamluk period and continued to be a feature of the city's housing during the later Ottoman period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-85">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond2000123,_157_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond2000123,_157-86">[84]</a></sup> These apartments were often laid out as multi-story <a href="/wiki/Duplex_(building)" title="Duplex (building)">duplexes</a> or triplexes. They were sometimes attached to caravanserais, where the two lower floors were for commercial and storage purposes and the multiple stories above them were rented out to tenants. The oldest partially-preserved example of this type of structure is the Wikala of Amir <a href="/wiki/Qawsun" title="Qawsun">Qawsun</a>, built before 1341.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-:02-85">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond2000123,_157_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond2000123,_157-86">[84]</a></sup> Residential buildings were in turn organized into close-knit neighbourhoods called a <i>harat</i>, which in many cases had gates that could be closed off at night or during disturbances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond2000123,_157_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond2000123,_157-86">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tombs_of_the_caliphs,_with_minaret,_Cairo,_Egypt._Coloured_l_Wellcome_V0049384.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3274" data-file-width="2150" decoding="async" height="335" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Tombs_of_the_caliphs%2C_with_minaret%2C_Cairo%2C_Egypt._Coloured_l_Wellcome_V0049384.jpg/220px-Tombs_of_the_caliphs%2C_with_minaret%2C_Cairo%2C_Egypt._Coloured_l_Wellcome_V0049384.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Tombs_of_the_caliphs%2C_with_minaret%2C_Cairo%2C_Egypt._Coloured_l_Wellcome_V0049384.jpg/330px-Tombs_of_the_caliphs%2C_with_minaret%2C_Cairo%2C_Egypt._Coloured_l_Wellcome_V0049384.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Tombs_of_the_caliphs%2C_with_minaret%2C_Cairo%2C_Egypt._Coloured_l_Wellcome_V0049384.jpg/440px-Tombs_of_the_caliphs%2C_with_minaret%2C_Cairo%2C_Egypt._Coloured_l_Wellcome_V0049384.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tombs_of_the_caliphs,_with_minaret,_Cairo,_Egypt._Coloured_l_Wellcome_V0049384.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_of_Sultan_al-Ashraf_Qaytbay" title="Complex of Sultan al-Ashraf Qaytbay">Funerary complex of Sultan Qaytbay</a>, built in 1470–1474 in the <a href="/wiki/City_of_the_Dead_(Cairo)" title="City of the Dead (Cairo)">Northern Cemetery</a> (seen in lithograph from 1848)</div></div></div>
<p>When the traveller <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Battuta" title="Ibn Battuta">Ibn Battuta</a> first came to Cairo in 1326, he described it as the principal district of Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBattuta182913_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBattuta182913-87">[85]</a></sup> When he passed through the area again on his return journey in 1348 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Death_in_Egypt" title="Black Death in Egypt">Black Death</a> was ravaging most major cities. He cited reports of thousands of deaths per day in Cairo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBattuta1829225_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBattuta1829225-89">[87]</a></sup> Although Cairo avoided <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>'s stagnation during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a>, it could not escape the Black Death, which struck the city more than fifty times between 1348 and 1517.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShoshan20024_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShoshan20024-90">[88]</a></sup> During its initial, and most deadly waves, approximately 200,000 people were killed by the plague,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEByrne2004104–105_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEByrne2004104–105-91">[89]</a></sup> and, by the 15th century, Cairo's population had been reduced to between 150,000 and 300,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShoshan20021_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShoshan20021-92">[90]</a></sup> The population decline was accompanied by a period of political instability between 1348 and 1412. It was nonetheless in this period that the largest Mamluk-era religious monument, the <a href="/wiki/Mosque-Madrasa_of_Sultan_Hassan" title="Mosque-Madrasa of Sultan Hassan">Madrasa-Mosque of Sultan Hasan</a>, was built.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond1993143–147_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond1993143–147-93">[91]</a></sup> In the late 14th century the <a href="/wiki/Burji_dynasty" title="Burji dynasty">Burji Mamluks</a> replaced the <a href="/wiki/Bahri_dynasty" title="Bahri dynasty">Bahri Mamluks</a> as rulers of the Mamluk state, but the Mamluk system continued to decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond1993169–177_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond1993169–177-94">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though the plagues returned frequently throughout the 15th century, Cairo remained a major metropolis and its population recovered in part through <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">rural migration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond1993169–177_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond1993169–177-94">[92]</a></sup> More conscious efforts were conducted by rulers and city officials to redress the city's infrastructure and cleanliness. Its economy and politics also became more deeply connected with the wider Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond1993169–177_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond1993169–177-94">[92]</a></sup> Some Mamluk sultans in this period, such as <a href="/wiki/Barsbay" title="Barsbay">Barbsay</a> (r. 1422–1438) and <a href="/wiki/Qaitbay" title="Qaitbay">Qaytbay</a> (r. 1468–1496), had relatively long and successful reigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClot1996183,_222–230_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClot1996183,_222–230-95">[93]</a></sup> After al-Nasir Muhammad, Qaytbay was one of the most prolific patrons of art and architecture of the Mamluk era. He built or restored numerous monuments in Cairo, in addition to commissioning projects beyond Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClot1996223_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClot1996223-96">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBehrens-Abouseif200769_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBehrens-Abouseif200769-97">[95]</a></sup> The crisis of Mamluk power and of Cairo's economic role deepened after Qaytbay. The city's status was diminished after <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a> discovered a sea route around the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a> between 1497 and 1499, thereby allowing spice traders to avoid Cairo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShillington2005199_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShillington2005199-83">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_rule">Ottoman rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cairo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ottoman rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Ottoman_Egypt" title="History of Ottoman Egypt">History of Ottoman Egypt</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali%27s_seizure_of_power" title="Muhammad Ali's seizure of power">Muhammad Ali's seizure of power</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Environs_du_Kaire_(Cairo)._Plan_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9ral_de_Boul%C3%A2q,_du_Kaire,_de_l%27%C3%AEle_de_Roudah_(el-R%C3%B4da),_du_Vieux_Kaire_et_de_Gyzeh_(J%C3%AEzah)_(NYPL_b14212718-1268726).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3885" data-file-width="5208" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Environs_du_Kaire_%28Cairo%29._Plan_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9ral_de_Boul%C3%A2q%2C_du_Kaire%2C_de_l%27%C3%AEle_de_Roudah_%28el-R%C3%B4da%29%2C_du_Vieux_Kaire_et_de_Gyzeh_%28J%C3%AEzah%29_%28NYPL_b14212718-1268726%29.jpg/220px-Environs_du_Kaire_%28Cairo%29._Plan_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9ral_de_Boul%C3%A2q%2C_du_Kaire%2C_de_l%27%C3%AEle_de_Roudah_%28el-R%C3%B4da%29%2C_du_Vieux_Kaire_et_de_Gyzeh_%28J%C3%AEzah%29_%28NYPL_b14212718-1268726%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Environs_du_Kaire_%28Cairo%29._Plan_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9ral_de_Boul%C3%A2q%2C_du_Kaire%2C_de_l%27%C3%AEle_de_Roudah_%28el-R%C3%B4da%29%2C_du_Vieux_Kaire_et_de_Gyzeh_%28J%C3%AEzah%29_%28NYPL_b14212718-1268726%29.jpg/330px-Environs_du_Kaire_%28Cairo%29._Plan_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9ral_de_Boul%C3%A2q%2C_du_Kaire%2C_de_l%27%C3%AEle_de_Roudah_%28el-R%C3%B4da%29%2C_du_Vieux_Kaire_et_de_Gyzeh_%28J%C3%AEzah%29_%28NYPL_b14212718-1268726%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Environs_du_Kaire_%28Cairo%29._Plan_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9ral_de_Boul%C3%A2q%2C_du_Kaire%2C_de_l%27%C3%AEle_de_Roudah_%28el-R%C3%B4da%29%2C_du_Vieux_Kaire_et_de_Gyzeh_%28J%C3%AEzah%29_%28NYPL_b14212718-1268726%29.jpg/440px-Environs_du_Kaire_%28Cairo%29._Plan_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9ral_de_Boul%C3%A2q%2C_du_Kaire%2C_de_l%27%C3%AEle_de_Roudah_%28el-R%C3%B4da%29%2C_du_Vieux_Kaire_et_de_Gyzeh_%28J%C3%AEzah%29_%28NYPL_b14212718-1268726%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Environs_du_Kaire_(Cairo)._Plan_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9ral_de_Boul%C3%A2q,_du_Kaire,_de_l%27%C3%AEle_de_Roudah_(el-R%C3%B4da),_du_Vieux_Kaire_et_de_Gyzeh_(J%C3%AEzah)_(NYPL_b14212718-1268726).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Cairo in 1809, from the <i><a href="/wiki/Description_de_l%27%C3%89gypte" title="Description de l'Égypte">Description de l'Égypte</a></i>.</div></div></div>
<p>Cairo's political influence diminished significantly after the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a> defeated <a href="/wiki/Al-Ashraf_Qansuh_al-Ghuri" title="Al-Ashraf Qansuh al-Ghuri">Sultan al-Ghuri</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marj_Dabiq" title="Battle of Marj Dabiq">Battle of Marj Dabiq</a> in 1516 and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Mamluk_War_(1516%E2%80%931517)" title="Ottoman–Mamluk War (1516–1517)">conquered Egypt</a> in 1517. Ruling from <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_sultans_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="List of sultans of the Ottoman Empire">Sultan</a> <a href="/wiki/Selim_I" title="Selim I">Selim I</a> relegated Egypt to a <a href="/wiki/Eyalet" title="Eyalet">province</a>, with Cairo as its capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShillington2005447_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShillington2005447-98">[96]</a></sup> For this reason, the history of Cairo during Ottoman times is often described as inconsequential, especially in comparison to other time periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShillington2005199_83-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShillington2005199-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinter1992225_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinter1992225-99">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup> However, during the 16th and 17th centuries, Cairo remained an important economic and cultural centre. Although no longer on the spice route, the city facilitated the transportation of <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemeni</a> <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a> <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">textiles</a>, primarily to <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>. Cairene merchants were instrumental in bringing goods to the barren <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a>, especially during the annual <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">hajj</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinter1992225_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinter1992225-99">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEİnalcıkFaroqhiQuataertMcGowan1997507–509_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEİnalcıkFaroqhiQuataertMcGowan1997507–509-101">[99]</a></sup> It was during this same period that <a href="/wiki/Al-Azhar_University" title="Al-Azhar University">al-Azhar University</a> reached the predominance among Islamic schools that it continues to hold today;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinter2004115_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinter2004115-102">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalyPetry199894–95_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalyPetry199894–95-103">[101]</a></sup> pilgrims on their way to hajj often attested to the superiority of the institution, which had become associated with Egypt's body of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulema" title="Ulema">Islamic scholars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinter2004115–117_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinter2004115–117-104">[102]</a></sup> The first printing press of the Middle East, printing in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a>, was established in Cairo <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1557</span> by a scion of the <a href="/wiki/Soncino_family_(printers)" title="Soncino family (printers)">Soncino</a> family of printers, <a href="/wiki/Italian_Jews" title="Italian Jews">Italian Jews</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jews" title="Ashkenazi Jews">Ashkenazi</a> origin who operated a press in Constantinople. The existence of the press is known solely from two fragments discovered in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cairo_Genizah" title="Cairo Genizah">Cairo Genizah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Comfort_Tiffany_-_On_the_Way_between_Old_and_New_Cairo,_Citadel_Mosque_of_Mohammed_Ali,_and_Tombs_of_the_Mamelukes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3487" data-file-width="5732" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Louis_Comfort_Tiffany_-_On_the_Way_between_Old_and_New_Cairo%2C_Citadel_Mosque_of_Mohammed_Ali%2C_and_Tombs_of_the_Mamelukes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Louis_Comfort_Tiffany_-_On_the_Way_between_Old_and_New_Cairo%2C_Citadel_Mosque_of_Mohammed_Ali%2C_and_Tombs_of_the_Mamelukes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Louis_Comfort_Tiffany_-_On_the_Way_between_Old_and_New_Cairo%2C_Citadel_Mosque_of_Mohammed_Ali%2C_and_Tombs_of_the_Mamelukes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Louis_Comfort_Tiffany_-_On_the_Way_between_Old_and_New_Cairo%2C_Citadel_Mosque_of_Mohammed_Ali%2C_and_Tombs_of_the_Mamelukes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Louis_Comfort_Tiffany_-_On_the_Way_between_Old_and_New_Cairo%2C_Citadel_Mosque_of_Mohammed_Ali%2C_and_Tombs_of_the_Mamelukes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Louis_Comfort_Tiffany_-_On_the_Way_between_Old_and_New_Cairo%2C_Citadel_Mosque_of_Mohammed_Ali%2C_and_Tombs_of_the_Mamelukes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Comfort_Tiffany_-_On_the_Way_between_Old_and_New_Cairo,_Citadel_Mosque_of_Mohammed_Ali,_and_Tombs_of_the_Mamelukes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Louis_Comfort_Tiffany" title="Louis Comfort Tiffany">Louis Comfort Tiffany</a> (1848–1933). <i>On the Way between Old and New Cairo, Citadel Mosque of Mohammed Ali, and Tombs of the Mamelukes,</i> 1872. Oil on canvas. <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum" title="Brooklyn Museum">Brooklyn Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>Under the Ottomans, Cairo expanded south and west from its nucleus around the Citadel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinter1992226_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinter1992226-106">[104]</a></sup> The city was the second-largest in the empire, behind Constantinople, and, although migration was not the primary source of Cairo's growth, twenty percent of its population at the end of the 18th century consisted of religious minorities and foreigners from around the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinter1992226–227_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinter1992226–227-107">[105]</a></sup> Still, when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I" title="Napoleon I">Napoleon</a> arrived in Cairo in 1798, the city's population was less than 300,000, forty percent lower than it was at the height of Mamluk—and Cairene—influence in the mid-14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShillington2005199_83-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShillington2005199-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinter1992226–227_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinter1992226–227-107">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Campaign_of_Egypt" title="Campaign of Egypt">French occupation</a> was short-lived as <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> and Ottoman forces, including a sizeable <a href="/wiki/Albanians" title="Albanians">Albanian</a> contingent, recaptured the country in 1801. Cairo itself <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Cairo" title="Siege of Cairo">was besieged</a> by a British and Ottoman force culminating with the French surrender on 22 June 1801.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESicker2001103_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESicker2001103-108">[106]</a></sup> The British vacated Egypt two years later, leaving the Ottomans, the Albanians, and the long-weakened <a href="/wiki/Mamluk" title="Mamluk">Mamluks</a> jostling for control of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESicker2001104_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESicker2001104-109">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAfaf_Lutfi_Sayyid-Marsot198439_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAfaf_Lutfi_Sayyid-Marsot198439-110">[108]</a></sup> Continued civil war allowed an Albanian named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Muhammad Ali Pasha</a> to ascend to the role of <a href="/wiki/Commander" title="Commander">commander</a> and eventually, with the approval of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulema" title="Ulema">religious establishment</a>, viceroy of Egypt in 1805.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESicker2001104–105_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESicker2001104–105-111">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">Modern era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cairo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Modern era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
